      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:239px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg/237px-Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg" width="237" height="365" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg/356px-Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg/474px-Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_University_Ezra_Stiles_Charter_large.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a> copy of the Brown University Charter of 1764
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><b>Brown University</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Classification_of_Institutions_of_Higher_Education#Doctorate-granting_Universities" title="Carnegie Classification of Institutions of Higher Education">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="Providence, Rhode Island">Providence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>. Founded in 1764 prior to <a href="/wiki/American_independence" title="American independence" class="mw-redirect">American independence</a> from the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> as the <i>College in the English Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations</i>, Brown is the third oldest institution of higher education in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Colleges" title="Colonial Colleges" class="mw-redirect">seventh oldest</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The university consists of <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_Brown_University" title="The College of Brown University">The College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graduate_School_of_Brown_University" title="Graduate School of Brown University" class="mw-redirect">Graduate School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpert_Medical_School" title="Alpert Medical School">Alpert Medical School</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Brown_University_School_of_Engineering" title="Brown University School of Engineering">School of Engineering</a>.</p> 
     <p>Brown was the first college in the nation to accept students regardless of religious affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Brown accepts 8.9% of undergraduate applicants, placing it among the world's most selective universities.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Brown's international programs are organized through the <a href="/wiki/Watson_Institute_for_International_Studies" title="Watson Institute for International Studies">Watson Institute for International Studies</a>. The New Curriculum, instituted in 1969, eliminated distribution requirements and allows any course to be taken on a satisfactory/no credit basis. In addition, there are no pluses or minuses in the letter grading system. The school has the oldest undergraduate <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> program in the Ivy League (1847). <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_College_(Brown_University)" title="Pembroke College (Brown University)" class="mw-redirect">Pembroke College</a>, Brown's women's college, merged with the university in 1971. While Brown is considered a small research university with 713 full-time faculty and 1,947 graduate students, five of its professors and two of its alumni have been honored as <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Laureates" title="Nobel Laureates" class="mw-redirect">Nobel Laureates</a>. The faculty added 100 new professors in the past 10 years under the Boldly Brown campaign.</p> 
     <p>Completed concentrations of undergraduates by area are Social Sciences (42%), Humanities (26%), Life Sciences (17%), and the Physical Sciences (14%).<sup id="cite_ref-brown.edu_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown.edu-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Brown's main campus is located on <a href="/wiki/College_Hill,_Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="College Hill, Providence, Rhode Island">College Hill</a> on the <a href="/wiki/East_Side_of_Providence" title="East Side of Providence" class="mw-redirect">East Side of Providence</a>. Several of the buildings on the Brown campus from its founding 18th century period through the 20th century offer fine representation of the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian style</a> of American colonial era architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The university's 37 varsity athletic teams are known as the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Bears" title="Brown Bears">Brown Bears</a>. The school colors are <a href="/wiki/Seal_brown" title="Seal brown">seal brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(color)" title="Cardinal (color)">cardinal red</a>, and white. Brown's <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a>, which dates back to 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The costumed mascot named &quot;Bruno&quot; frequently makes appearances at athletic games. People associated with the University are known as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Brown_University_people" title="List of Brown University people">Brunonians</a>.</p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Brown_University" title="History of Brown University">History of Brown University</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:176px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:James_Manning_head_by_Cosmo_Alexander.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3e/James_Manning_head_by_Cosmo_Alexander.JPG" width="174" height="148" class="thumbimage" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:James_Manning_head_by_Cosmo_Alexander.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> First Brown President and Delegate to the 
        <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/James_Manning_(minister)" title="James Manning (minister)">James Manning</a>. Detail from an oil painting by 
        <a href="/wiki/Cosmo_Alexander" title="Cosmo Alexander">Cosmo Alexander</a>, 1770; in University Hall, Brown University, Providence, R.I.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Brown owes its founding to the support of learning among a <a href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist" class="mw-redirect">Baptist</a> Church association but in 1762, the Baptist Minister <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Edwards" title="Morgan Edwards">Morgan Edwards</a> was at first ridiculed for suggesting the founding of a college. In his <i>Materials for a History of the Baptists in Rhode Island</i>, Edwards wrote:&quot;The first mover [Edwards, himself] for it [a Baptist college] in 1762 was laughed at as a projector of a thing impracticable. Nay, many of the Baptists themselves discouraged the design (prophesying evil to the churches in case it should take place) from an unhappy prejudice against learning.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, Edwards joined several others as an original fellow or trustee for the chartering of the <i>College in the English Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations</i> (the former name for Brown University), the first Baptist college in the original <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_colonies" title="Thirteen colonies" class="mw-redirect">thirteen colonies</a>, and now one of the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> universities.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1763, The Reverend <a href="/wiki/James_Manning_(minister)" title="James Manning (minister)">James Manning</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist" class="mw-redirect">Baptist</a> minister, and an alumnus of the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">College of New Jersey</a> (predecessor to today's <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>), was sent to Rhode Island by the Philadelphia Association of Baptist Churches in order to found the college.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time, local <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalists</a>, led by the theologist <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>, were working toward a similar end. The inaugural board meeting of the Corporation of the College in the English <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Rhode_Island_and_Providence_Plantations" title="Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations">Colony of Rhode Island &amp; Providence Plantations</a> was held in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Colony_House" title="Old Colony House">Old Colony House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>. Former <a href="/wiki/List_of_colonial_governors_of_Rhode_Island" title="List of colonial governors of Rhode Island">colonial governors of Rhode Island</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hopkins_(politician)" title="Stephen Hopkins (politician)">Stephen Hopkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Ward_(American_statesman)" title="Samuel Ward (American statesman)">Samuel Ward</a>, as well as the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Backus" title="Isaac Backus">Isaac Backus</a> and the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Stillman" title="Samuel Stillman">Samuel Stillman</a>, were among those who played an instrumental role in Brown's foundation and later became American revolutionaries. On 3 March 1764, a charter was filed to create the College in <a href="/wiki/Warren,_Rhode_Island" title="Warren, Rhode Island">Warren, Rhode Island</a>, reflecting the work of both Stiles and Manning. The college gained its <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.brown.edu/Facilities/University_Library/exhibits/education/charter.html">charter</a> by grant of <a href="/wiki/King_George_III" title="King George III" class="mw-redirect">King George III</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg/220px-BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg/330px-BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg/440px-BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-UniversityHall1795.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> This copper-plate engraving from 1795 is the first known image of Brown. The original campus was a single academic building that was later named 
        <a href="/wiki/University_Hall_(Brown_University)" title="University Hall (Brown University)">University Hall</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The charter had more than sixty signatories, including the brothers <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(Rhode_Island)" title="John Brown (Rhode Island)">John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brown_(Brown_University)" title="Nicholas Brown (Brown University)" class="mw-redirect">Nicholas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moses_Brown" title="Moses Brown">Moses</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chad_Brown_(minister)" title="Chad Brown (minister)">Brown</a> family, who would later inspire the College's modern name following a gift bestowed by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brown,_Jr." title="Nicholas Brown, Jr.">Nicholas Brown, Jr.</a> The college's mission, the charter stated, was to prepare students &quot;for discharging the Offices of Life with usefulness &amp; reputation&quot; by providing instruction &quot;in the Vernacular and Learned Languages, and in the liberal Arts and Sciences.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The charter required that the makeup of the board of 36 trustees include, 22 <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, five <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends" class="mw-redirect">Friends</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalists</a>, and five <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England members</a>, and by 12 Fellows, of whom eight, including the President, should be Baptists &quot;and the rest indifferently of any or all denominations.&quot; It specified that &quot;into this liberal and catholic institution shall never be admitted any religious tests, but on the contrary, all the members hereof shall forever enjoy full, free, absolute, and uninterrupted liberty of conscience.&quot; One of the Baptist founders, <a href="/wiki/John_Gano" title="John Gano">John Gano</a>, had also been the founding minister of the <a href="/wiki/First_Baptist_Church_in_the_City_of_New_York" title="First Baptist Church in the City of New York">First Baptist Church in the City of New York</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclop&aelig;dia Britannica Eleventh Edition">Encyclop&aelig;dia Britannica Eleventh Edition</a> remarks that &quot;At the time it was framed the charter was considered extraordinarily <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a>&quot; and that &quot;the government has always been largely non-sectarian in spirit.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> In commemoration of this history, each spring faculty and the graduating class proceed down the hill, in academic dress, to the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/First_Baptist_Church_in_America" title="First Baptist Church in America">First Baptist Meeting House</a> (erected in 1774, &quot;for the publick Worship of Almighty GOD and also for holding Commencement in&quot;) for the conferral of the bachelors degree.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>James Manning was sworn in as the College's first president in 1765. His tenure lasted until 1791. During his presidency, the College moved to its present location on <a href="/wiki/College_Hill,_Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="College Hill, Providence, Rhode Island">College Hill</a> in the East Side of Providence in 1770 and construction of the first building, the College Edifice, began. This building was renamed University Hall in 1823.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Manning_Hall,_Brown_University,_Providence,_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg/220px-Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg/330px-Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg/440px-Manning_Hall%2C_Brown_University%2C_Providence%2C_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Manning_Hall,_Brown_University,_Providence,_Rhode_Island_-_20091108.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Hope College (left) was built in 1822, while Manning Hall (right) was built in 1834.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Brown family&nbsp;? <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brown_(Brown_University)" title="Nicholas Brown (Brown University)" class="mw-redirect">Nicholas Sr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(Rhode_Island)" title="John Brown (Rhode Island)">John</a>, Joseph, and <a href="/wiki/Moses_Brown" title="Moses Brown">Moses</a>,? were instrumental in the move to Providence, funding and organizing much of the construction of the new buildings on the former <a href="/wiki/Chad_Brown_(minister)" title="Chad Brown (minister)">Rev. Chad Brown</a> farm. The family's connection with the college was strong: Joseph Brown became a professor of Physics at the University, and John Brown served as treasurer from 1775 to 1796. In 1804, a year after John Brown's death, the University was renamed Brown University in honor of John's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brown,_Jr." title="Nicholas Brown, Jr.">Nicholas Brown, Jr.</a>, who was a member of the class of 1786 and in 1804 contributed $5,000 toward an endowed professorship. In 1904, the <a href="/wiki/John_Carter_Brown_Library" title="John Carter Brown Library">John Carter Brown Library</a> was opened as a research center on Americas based on the libraries of <a href="/wiki/John_Carter_Brown" title="John Carter Brown">John Carter Brown</a> and his son John Nicholas Brown. The Brown family was involved in various business ventures in Rhode Island, and made a small part of its wealth in businesses related to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">slave trade</a>. The family itself was divided on the issue. John Brown had unapologetically defended slavery, while Moses Brown and Nicholas Brown Jr. were fervent <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolitionists</a>. In recognition of this complex history, under President <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Simmons" title="Ruth Simmons">Ruth Simmons</a>, the University established the University Steering Committee on Slavery and Justice in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Brown began to admit women when it established a <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_College_in_Brown_University" title="Women's College in Brown University" class="mw-redirect">Women's College in Brown University</a> in 1891, which was later named <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_College_in_Brown_University" title="Pembroke College in Brown University">Pembroke College in Brown University</a>. &quot;The College&quot; (the undergraduate school) merged with Pembroke College in 1971 and became co-educational.</p> 
     <p>The language of the Brown University charter has long been interpreted by the university as discouraging the founding of a <a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">business school</a> or <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law school</a>. Brown continues to be one of only two Ivy League colleges with neither a business school nor a law school (the other being Princeton). Nevertheless, Brown recently developed an Executive MBA program in conjunction with one of the leading Business Schools in Europe; <a href="/wiki/IE_Business_School" title="IE Business School">IE Business School</a> in Madrid.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> In this partnership, Brown provides the traditional coursework while IE provides most of the business related subjects.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Revolution">American Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: American Revolution">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Declaration_independence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Declaration_independence.jpg/220px-Declaration_independence.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Declaration_independence.jpg/330px-Declaration_independence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Declaration_independence.jpg/440px-Declaration_independence.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Declaration_independence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>'s 
        <i>Declaration of Independence.</i> The signers included Brown's first chancellor 
        <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hopkins_(politician)" title="Stephen Hopkins (politician)">Stephen Hopkins</a>. Hopkins wears a hat and stands in the back of the room near the door on the left.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hopkins_(politician)" title="Stephen Hopkins (politician)">Stephen Hopkins</a>, Chief Justice and <a href="/wiki/Governor" title="Governor">Governor</a> of the <i>English Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations</i>, was a Delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Albany_Congress" title="Albany Congress">Colonial Congress in Albany</a> in 1754 and to the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> from 1774 to 1776. He was a signatory to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a> on behalf of the state of Rhode Island. He also served as the first chancellor of Brown from 1764 to 1785. His house is a minor historical site, located just off the main green at Brown.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:W-RSimpleMap.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/W-RSimpleMap.gif/170px-W-RSimpleMap.gif" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/W-RSimpleMap.gif/255px-W-RSimpleMap.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/W-RSimpleMap.gif/340px-W-RSimpleMap.gif 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:W-RSimpleMap.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> National Park Service map of the 
        <a href="/wiki/Washington%E2%80%93Rochambeau_Revolutionary_Route" title="Washington?Rochambeau Revolutionary Route">Washington?Rochambeau Revolutionary Route</a> (W3R)
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1781, allied American and French armies under the command of General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Comte_de_Rochambeau" title="Comte de Rochambeau" class="mw-redirect">Comte de Rochambeau</a>, who led troops sent by King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI of France</a>, embarked on a 600-mile (970&nbsp;km) <a href="/wiki/Washington-Rochambeau_Revolutionary_Route" title="Washington-Rochambeau Revolutionary Route" class="mw-redirect">march</a> from Rhode Island to <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, where they fought and defeated British forces sent by King <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III of the United Kingdom</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia" title="Yorktown, Virginia">Yorktown, Virginia</a> peninsula. The victory ended the major battles of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. Prior to the march, Brown University served as an encampment site for French troops, and the College Edifice, now University Hall, was turned into a military hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Other founders of Brown who played significant roles in the American revolutionary effort included <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(Rhode_Island)" title="John Brown (Rhode Island)">John Brown</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gaspee_Affair" title="Gaspee Affair" class="mw-redirect">Gaspee Affair</a>, Chief Justice Dr. <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Babcock" title="Joshua Babcock">Joshua Babcock</a> as major general in the state militia and <a href="/wiki/William_Ellery" title="William Ellery">William Ellery</a> as a signatory to the Declaration of Independence.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/James_Mitchell_Varnum" title="James Mitchell Varnum">James Mitchell Varnum</a>, who graduated with honors in Brown's first graduating class of 1769, served as one of General George Washington's <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> Brigadier Generals and later as Major General in command of the entire <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_militia" title="Rhode Island militia" class="mw-redirect">Rhode Island militia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Howell_(jurist)" title="David Howell (jurist)">David Howell</a>, who graduated with an A.M. in the same year as General Varnum, served as a delegate to the Continental Congress from 1782 to 1785.</p> 
     <p>In 1786, President James Manning was elected and served as representative for Rhode Island to the seventh session of the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">United States in Congress Assembled</a>, held in <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a>. In that capacity, he served on the Grand Committee, which proposed fundamental amendments to the Articles of Confederation and presaged the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Constitution of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_curriculum">New curriculum</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: New curriculum">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 1850, Brown President <a href="/wiki/Francis_Wayland" title="Francis Wayland">Francis Wayland</a> wrote: &quot;The various courses should be so arranged that, insofar as practicable, every student might study what he chose, all that he chose, and nothing but what he chose.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1969, the adoption of the New Curriculum marked a milestone in the University's institutional history and is seen as a significant step towards realizing Wayland's vision. The <a href="/wiki/Curriculum" title="Curriculum">curriculum</a> was the result of a paper written by <a href="/wiki/Ira_Magaziner" title="Ira Magaziner">Ira Magaziner</a> and Elliot Maxwell entitled &quot;Draft of a Working Paper for Education at Brown University.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The paper came out of a year-long Group Independent Study Project (GISP) involving 80 students and 15 professors. The group was inspired by student-initiated experimental schools, especially <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_State_University" title="San Francisco State University">San Francisco State College</a>, and sought ways to improve education for students at Brown. The philosophy they formed sought to &quot;put students at the center of their education&quot; and to &quot;teach students how to think rather than just teaching facts.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-bdh_newcurr_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bdh_newcurr-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg/170px-BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg/255px-BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg/340px-BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-Poster1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Poster celebrating Brown's 150 year anniversary in 1914
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The paper made a number of suggestions for improving education at Brown, including a new kind of <a href="/wiki/Interdisciplinary" title="Interdisciplinary" class="mw-redirect">interdisciplinary</a> freshman course that would introduce new modes of inquiry and bring faculty from different fields together. Their goal was to transform the survey course, which traditionally sought to cover a large amount of basic material, into specialized courses that would introduce the important modes of inquiry used in different disciplines.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Following a student rally in support of reform, President Ray Heffner appointed the Special Committee on Curricular Philosophy with the task of developing specific reforms. These reforms, known as the Maeder Report (after the chair of the committee), were then brought to the faculty for a vote. On 7 May 1969, following a marathon meeting with 260 faculty members present, the New Curriculum was passed. Its key features included the following:<sup id="cite_ref-bdh_newcurr_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bdh_newcurr-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Modes of Thought courses aimed at first-year students</li> 
      <li>Interdisciplinary University courses</li> 
      <li>Students could elect to take any course Satisfactory/No Credit</li> 
      <li>Distribution requirements were dropped</li> 
      <li>The University simplified grades to ABC/No Credit, eliminating pluses, minuses and D's. Furthermore, &quot;No Credit&quot; would not appear on external transcripts.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Except for the Modes of Thought courses, a key component of the reforms which have been discontinued, these elements of the New Curriculum are still in place.</p> 
     <p>Additionally, due to the school's proximity and close partnership with the <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_School_of_Design" title="Rhode Island School of Design">Rhode Island School of Design</a> (RISD), Brown students have the opportunity to take up to four courses at RISD and have the credit count towards a Brown degree. Likewise, RISD students can also take courses at Brown. Since the two campuses are effectively adjacent to each other, the two institutions often partner to provide both student bodies with services (such as the local Brown/RISD after-hours and downtown transportation shuttles).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 2007 the two institutions announced the formation of the Brown/RISD Dual Degree Program, which allowed students to pursue an A.B. degree at Brown and a B.F.A. degree at RISD simultaneously, a five-year program. The first students in the new program matriculated in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG/220px-Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG/330px-Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG/440px-Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_University_seal_building_detail.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Brown University seal as a detail on a university building
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>As recently as 2006, there has been some debate on reintroducing plus/minus grading to the curriculum. Advocates argue that adding pluses and minuses would reduce <a href="/wiki/Grade_inflation" title="Grade inflation">grade inflation</a> and allow professors to give more specific grades,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> while critics say that this plan would have no effect on grade inflation while increasing unnecessary competition among students and violating the principle of the New Curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Ultimately, the addition of pluses and minuses to the grading system was voted down by the College Curriculum Council.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University is currently in the process of broadening and expanding its curricular offerings as part of the &quot;Plan for Academic Enrichment.&quot; The number of faculty has been greatly expanded. Seminars aimed at freshmen have begun to be offered widely by most departments.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>As a part of the re-accreditation process, Brown University is undergoing an expansive reevaluation of its undergraduate education offerings through the newly appointed Task Force on Undergraduate Education. This Task Force is charged with assessing the areas of general education, concentrations, advising, and pedagogy and assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidents">Presidents</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Presidents">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Brown_University" title="List of Presidents of Brown University">List of Presidents of Brown University</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:DrRuthJSimmons.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/DrRuthJSimmons.jpg/170px-DrRuthJSimmons.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/DrRuthJSimmons.jpg/255px-DrRuthJSimmons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/DrRuthJSimmons.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:DrRuthJSimmons.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 18th Brown President 
        <a href="/wiki/Ruth_J._Simmons" title="Ruth J. Simmons" class="mw-redirect">Ruth J. Simmons</a>, 2001&nbsp;? 2012.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:JonathanMaxcy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/JonathanMaxcy.jpg/170px-JonathanMaxcy.jpg" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/JonathanMaxcy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/JonathanMaxcy.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:JonathanMaxcy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Brown's second President, 
        <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Maxcy" title="Jonathan Maxcy">Jonathan Maxcy</a>, 1792?1802, was also an alumnus (A.B. 1787).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The immediate past president of the University was Ruth J. Simmons. She was the 18th president of Brown University and first <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> president of an Ivy League institution. According to a November 2007 poll by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brown_Daily_Herald" title="The Brown Daily Herald">The Brown Daily Herald</a></i>, Simmons enjoyed a more than 80% approval rating among Brown undergraduates.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> On 15 September 2011, Simmons announced her plans to step down as president at the end of her term in June 2012. She has plans to remain at Brown as a professor of Comparative Literature and Africana Studies.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> On 2 March 2012 it was officially announced that Dr. <a href="/wiki/Christina_Hull_Paxson" title="Christina Hull Paxson" class="mw-redirect">Christina Hull Paxson</a> would replace Simmons and become the 19th president of Brown University as of 1 July 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_College">The College</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The College">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_Brown_University" title="The College of Brown University">The College of Brown University</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/220px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/330px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/440px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Robinson Hall, now home to the Department of Economics, was the main university library in the 1800s.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Founded in 1764, The College is the oldest school of Brown University. Nearly 6,000 undergraduate students are currently enrolled in the college, and approximately 80 concentrations (majors) are offered. Completed concentrations of undergraduates by area are Social Sciences (42%), Humanities (26%), Life Sciences (17%), and the Physical Sciences (14%).<sup id="cite_ref-brown.edu_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown.edu-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The concentrations with the greatest number of students are Biology, History, and International Relations.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Brown is one of the few schools in the United States with an undergraduate concentration (major) in <a href="/wiki/Egyptology" title="Egyptology">Egyptology</a>. Undergraduates can also design an independent concentration if the existing standard programs do not fit their interests.</p> 
     <p>35% of undergraduates pursue graduate or professional study immediately, 60% within 5 years, and 80% within 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-admit-facts_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admit-facts-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> For the Class of 1998, 75% of all graduates enrolled in a graduate or professional degree program since graduating from Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The most degrees acquired were a Master's (35%) in any field, a Medical (28%), Doctoral (22%), or a Law (14%) degree, in highest order.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> There are not many who pursued the Ph.D compared to a Master's (for instance, business), or a Medical degree.</p> 
     <p>The highest fields of employment for graduates of the College are Business (36%), Education (19%), Health/Medical (6%), Arts (6%), Government (6%), and Communications/Media (5%) in highest order.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graduate_School">Graduate School</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Graduate School">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_University_Graduate_School" title="Brown University Graduate School">Brown University Graduate School</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Established in 1887, The Graduate School currently houses around 2,000 students studying over 50 disciplines. 20 different <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degrees" title="Master's degrees" class="mw-redirect">master's degrees</a> are offered as well as <a href="/wiki/Ph.D." title="Ph.D." class="mw-redirect">Ph.D.</a> degrees in over 40 subjects ranging from <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Public_policy" title="Public policy">public policy</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpert_Medical_School">Alpert Medical School</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Alpert Medical School">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Alpert_Medical_School" title="Alpert Medical School">Alpert Medical School</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG/200px-Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG" width="200" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG/300px-Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG/400px-Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Brown_Alpert_Medical_School_Med-Ed_Building.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Med-Ed Building: The new home of Brown Alpert Medical School
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University's medical program started in 1811, but the school was suspended by President Wayland in 1827 after the program's faculty declined to live on campus (a new requirement under Wayland). In 1975, the first M.D. degrees from the new Program in Medicine were awarded to a graduating class of 58 students. In 1991, the school was officially renamed the Brown University School of Medicine, then renamed once more to Brown Medical School in October 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In January 2007, self-made entrepreneur Warren Alpert donated $100 million to Brown Medical School on behalf of the Warren Alpert Foundation, tying <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Frank" title="Sidney Frank">Sidney Frank</a> for the largest single monetary contribution ever made to the University. In recognition of the gift, the faculty of Brown University approved changing the name of the Brown Medical School to The Warren Alpert Medical School of Brown University. It is currently ranked 29th among U.S. medical schools in research and 28th in primary care according to <a href="/wiki/US_News_and_World_Report" title="US News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">US News and World Report</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Admissions to Alpert is one of the most competitive in the nation, with only less than 2% of those applying through the Standard Route accepted in 2008 (5,902 applications for 94 spots).</p> 
     <p>The medical school is known for its eight-year <a href="/wiki/Program_in_Liberal_Medical_Education" title="Program in Liberal Medical Education" class="mw-redirect">Program in Liberal Medical Education</a> (PLME), which was started in 1984 and is one of the most selective programs in the nation. Each year, approximately 60 high school students matriculate into the PLME out of an applicant pool of about 1,600. Since 1976, the Early Identification Program (EIP) has encouraged Rhode Island residents to pursue careers in medicine by recruiting sophomores from <a href="/wiki/Providence_College" title="Providence College">Providence College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_College" title="Rhode Island College">Rhode Island College</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rhode_Island" title="University of Rhode Island">University of Rhode Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tougaloo_College" title="Tougaloo College">Tougaloo College</a>. In 2004, the school once again began to accept applications from premedical students at other colleges and universities via <a href="/wiki/AMCAS" title="AMCAS" class="mw-redirect">AMCAS</a> like most other medical schools. The medical school also offers combined degree programs leading to the M.D./<a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a>, M.D./<a href="/wiki/Master_of_Public_Health" title="Master of Public Health" class="mw-redirect">M.P.H.</a> and M.D./<a href="/wiki/Master_of_Public_Policy" title="Master of Public Policy">M.P.P.</a> degrees.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="School_of_Engineering">School of Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: School of Engineering">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_University_School_of_Engineering" title="Brown University School of Engineering">Brown University School of Engineering</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Brown has the oldest engineering program in the Ivy League. In 1916, multiple Departments of Engineering were consolidated into the Division of Engineering. As part of a broader growth initiative, in 2010, the Division of Engineering was authorized to begin operating as the School of Engineering.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_centers_and_institutes">Research centers and institutes</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Research centers and institutes">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_Biological_Laboratory">Marine Biological Laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Marine Biological Laboratory">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Marine_Biological_Laboratory" title="Marine Biological Laboratory">Marine Biological Laboratory</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Marine_Biological_Laboratory" title="Marine Biological Laboratory">Marine Biological Laboratory</a> (MBL) is an independent research institution established in 1882 at Woods Hole, Massachusetts. The laboratory is linked to 54 current or past <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Laureates" title="Nobel Laureates" class="mw-redirect">Nobel Laureates</a> at its research or teaching faculty. Since 2005, the MBL and Brown University have collaborated in a Ph.D.-awarding Graduate Program in Biological and Environmental Sciences, which brings together faculty from both institutions. In 2010, the joint program was extended to include more professors participating in the partnership. The current faculty includes those from the Ecosystems Center, the Bay Paul Center, the Program in Cellular Dynamics, and the Marine Resources Center.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Watson_Institute_for_International_Studies">Watson Institute for International Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Watson Institute for International Studies">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Watson_Institute_for_International_Studies" title="Watson Institute for International Studies">Watson Institute for International Studies</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg/220px-BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg/330px-BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg/440px-BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-WatsonInstitute.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Watson_Institute_for_International_Studies" title="Watson Institute for International Studies">Watson Institute</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Watson Institute for International Studies, usually referred to as the Watson Institute, is a center for the analysis of international issues at Brown University. Its original benefactor was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Watson,_Jr." title="Thomas Watson, Jr.">Thomas Watson, Jr.</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Ambassador_to_the_Soviet_Union" title="Ambassador to the Soviet Union" class="mw-redirect">Ambassador to the Soviet Union</a> and president of <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pembroke_Center_for_Teaching_and_Research_on_Women">Pembroke Center for Teaching and Research on Women</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pembroke Center for Teaching and Research on Women">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_Center_for_Teaching_and_Research_on_Women" title="Pembroke Center for Teaching and Research on Women">Pembroke Center for Teaching and Research on Women</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The Pembroke Center for Teaching and Research on Women, was established at Brown in 1981 as a research center on gender. It was named in honor of Pembroke College (Brown University), a decade after its merger with Brown University, and to recognize the history of women's efforts to gain access to higher education. Along with its numerous academic programs, including its sponsorship of post-doctoral research fellowships, the undergraduate concentration in <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">Gender</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_sexuality" title="Human sexuality">Sexuality</a> Studies, and the annual Pembroke Seminar, the Pembroke Center also organizes a number of programs throughout the year to recognize the historical achievements of women. Leading historian and social scientist <a href="/wiki/Joan_Wallach_Scott" title="Joan Wallach Scott">Joan Wallach Scott</a> was founding director of the Center. The Pembroke Center is affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Doyle_Women%27s_Center" title="Sarah Doyle Women's Center">Sarah Doyle Women's Center</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computing">Computing</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Computing">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Several projects of note involving hypertext and other forms of electronic text have been developed at Brown, including <a href="/wiki/FRESS" title="FRESS" class="mw-redirect">FRESS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_University_Interactive_Language" title="Brown University Interactive Language">Brown University Interactive Language</a> (BRUIN), <a href="/wiki/Hypertext_Editing_System" title="Hypertext Editing System">Hypertext Editing System</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women_Writers_Project" title="Women Writers Project">Women Writers Project</a>. In addition, the Computer Science department at Brown is home to <a href="/wiki/Cave_Automatic_Virtual_Environment" title="Cave Automatic Virtual Environment" class="mw-redirect">The CAVE</a>. This project is a complete <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> room, one of few in the world, and is used for everything from three-dimensional drawing classes to tours of the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> for medical students. In 2000, a group of students from the university's Technology House converted the south side of the Sciences Library into a giant video display which allowed bystanders to play <a href="/wiki/Tetris" title="Tetris">Tetris</a>, the largest of its kind ever in the Western Hemisphere. Constructed from 11 custom-built circuit boards, a 12-story data network, a <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a> running <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, a radio-frequency video game controller, and over 10,000 Christmas lights, the project was named La Bastille and could be seen for several miles.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

     <p><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> ranked Brown 15th among national universities in its 2012 edition (published in August 2012). Undergraduates make up a larger proportion of the student body at Brown than at any of the other Ivy League universities, leading some Brown administrators to question the fairness of the ranking in light of the University's focus on undergraduate education.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The same edition also ranked Brown 4th among national universities for undergraduate teaching (tying with <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Internationally, in 2012 Brown ranked 51st in the <a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a> ranked Brown 65th. In 2012 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a> placed Brown at 42nd.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the 2005 paper <i>Academic Ranking of World Universities ? Methodologies and Problems</i> by the rankers, Brown's PCP (performance per-capita) score is equal to that of <u><a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, at 32.1, or 28th in the world</u>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the 2011-2012 Times Higher Education Supplement ranking, Brown University's citation per capita score, counting for 30% of the overall score, is <u>93.9, or 32nd of the Top 200 universities, tied with <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College London</a></u>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>click on &quot;change criteria&quot;</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Brown ranked 7th in the country (between <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a> and Columbia) in a study of high school seniors' <a href="/wiki/Revealed_preferences" title="Revealed preferences" class="mw-redirect">revealed preferences</a> for matriculation conducted by economists at Harvard, Wharton, and Boston University, and published in 2005 by the <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research" title="National Bureau of Economic Research">National Bureau of Economic Research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2008 Center for College Affordability and Productivity (CCAP) ranked Brown 5th in the country among national universities.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em"> 
      <tbody>

        <td>51</td> 
       </tr>
